WebSync metadata title: Threat Modeling as Code: Implementing STRIDE in DevSecOps | by Urshila | Medium url: https://medium.com/@urshilaravindran/threat-modeling-as-code-implementing-stride-in-devsecops-0dd9f9ff06c1 date: 2026-05-30T19:43:04.695Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap With the rise of DevSecOps, security is no longer an afterthought but an integral part of the development lifecycle. Traditional security approaches often slow down deployment, but integrating Threat Modeling as Code (TMAC) ensures that security assessments are automated, scalable, and continuous. STRIDE, a threat classification framework developed by Microsoft, plays a crucial role in DevSecOps threat modeling by identifying security weaknesses at every stage of software development. This blog covers the implementation of STRIDE within DevSecOps — how to automate security reviews, integrate with CI/CD pipelines, and proactively mitigate threats before production.
What is Threat Modeling? Threat modeling is a structured approach to identifying, enumerating, and prioritizing potential threats in an application, system, or architecture. Why is Threat Modeling Important? Early Risk Identification: Finds security flaws before attackers do. Cost Reduction: Fixing security issues early is cheaper than after deployment. Better Compliance: Helps in meeting regulatory requirements (e.g., GDPR, HIPAA, PCI-DSS). Secure Design: Ensures that security is integrated at the design stage. Common Threat Modeling Approaches STRIDE (Microsoft model) PASTA (Process for Attack Simulation and Threat Analysis) DREAD (Risk assessment methodology) OCTAVE (Operationally Critical Threat, Asset, and Vulnerability Evaluation) VAST (Visual, Agile, and Simple Threat Modeling) Among these, STRIDE is one of the most widely used models in DevSecOps threat modeling.
What is STRIDE? STRIDE is a threat classification model developed by Microsoft that categorizes security threats into six key areas: Why is STRIDE Relevant to Threat Modeling? STRIDE systematically identifies security weaknesses in a system by focusing on different attack vectors, allowing security teams to: Identify vulnerabilities before coding starts. Integrate security within DevSecOps pipelines. Automate security analysis using Threat Modeling as Code.
What is DevSecOps? DevSecOps (Development, Security, Operations) DevSecOps is a cultural and technical shift that integrates security practices into DevOps workflows. Unlike traditional “security at the end” approaches, DevSecOps ensures that security is: Automated in the CI/CD pipeline. Continuous throughout the SDLC. Collaborative, involving developers, security teams, and operations. Key Principles of DevSecOps Shift Left Security — Implement security early in development. Automation — Use security tools in CI/CD pipelines. Continuous Monitoring — Detect threats in real-time. Threat Modeling as Code — Automate STRIDE threat modeling within DevOps.
What Role Does STRIDE Play in DevSecOps? STRIDE fits seamlessly into DevSecOps by: Identifying security risks before development starts. Automating threat modeling using scripts and tools. Ensuring compliance by detecting security flaws in CI/CD pipelines. Integrating security scans alongside DevOps processes. Integrating STRIDE with DevSecOps By integrating STRIDE into Threat Modeling as Code (TMAC), DevSecOps teams can automate security reviews, generate security reports, and track vulnerabilities in real-time.
How to Use STRIDE in Threat Modeling? Steps to Implement STRIDE Threat Modeling in DevSecOps <h3,>Step 1: Define the Application Architecture & Attack Surface Identify all system components: APIs, databases, services, microservices. Document trust boundaries: Internal, external, third-party services. Tools: Draw.io, OWASP Threat Dragon, Microsoft Threat Modeling Tool. Step 2: Identify STRIDE Threats for each component Use STRIDE categories to map threats to application components. Example for an API-based app: STRIDE for an API-Based Application Spoofing → API keys stolen. Tampering → Modifying JWT tokens. Repudiation → No logging for failed authentication. Information Disclosure → Unprotected user data. Denial of Service → API request floods. Elevation of Privilege → Privilege escalation via insecure endpoints. Step 3: Automate STRIDE Threat Modeling with Code Use Threat Modeling as Code (TMAC) to automate STRIDE threat detection. Example using PyTM (Python Threat Modeling Framework)  for STRIDE Threat Modeling: This script: Defines system components (server, database). Establishes data flow (API calls). Identifies STRIDE threats (lack of encryption, authentication). Benefits: Automates STRIDE threat detection.
Generates security reports for developers.
Integrates with DevSecOps pipelines (GitHub Actions, Jenkins). Step 4: Integrate STRIDE Threat Modeling in CI/CD GitHub Actions / Jenkins: Run automated threat modeling checks. DevSecOps Tools: OWASP Threat Dragon — Visual threat modeling. PyTM — Python-based threat modeling. Threatspec — Embed security into DevOps pipelines. Microsoft Threat Modeling Tool — GUI-based modeling. Example of integrating PyTM in a CI/CD pipeline (GitHub Actions):
Automates security analysis on every code push. Blocks deployments with critical security flaws. Generates security reports for remediation. Step 5: Implement STRIDE-Based Security Testing Automate STRIDE security tests within CI/CD. Tools: OWASP ZAP, Burp Suite, Nikto, SonarQube, Snyk. Step 6: Continuous Monitoring & Improvement Integrate with SIEM (Splunk, ELK Stack). Conduct regular threat modeling sessions for new features. Refine STRIDE rules based on real-world threats. </h3,> CONCLUSION: Why STRIDE Threat Modeling as Code is a Game-Changer for DevSecOps
Automates security reviews using Threat Modeling as Code (TMAC).
Detects risks early in the development lifecycle.
Integrates with CI/CD pipelines to prevent security misconfigurations.
Provides real-time security insights via automated threat modeling reports.
Enhances collaboration between security, development, and DevOps teams. By leveraging STRIDE in Threat Modeling as Code, organizations can secure applications at scale, reducing vulnerabilities and improving compliance without slowing down development. Source: 2026-05-30 A Framework for Converging Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) and Vulnerability Operations Centers (VOC) | by sylvain ridoux | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: A Framework for Converging Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) and Vulnerability Operations Centers (VOC) | by sylvain ridoux | Medium url: https://medium.com/@sylvain.ridoux/a-framework-for-converging-threat-modeling-as-code-tmac-and-vulnerability-operations-centers-2429f7499b2e date: 2026-05-30T19:42:46.313Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Abstract In modern software development ecosystems, characterized by continuous deployments and Infrastructure as Code (IaC), security practices must evolve beyond traditional paradigms. This paper proposes a novel framework for integrating Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) with next-generation Vulnerability Operations Centers (VOC) to create anr autonomous and adaptive security intelligence system. This convergence aims to transform risk management from a reactive, fragmented process into a predictive, holistic, and automated system. We will explore the limitations of current approaches, present the core principles of our proposed framework, and discuss its potential impact on security operations and organizational structure. Keywords: Threat Modeling, DevSecOps, Vulnerability Management, Security Automation, Infrastructure as Code, MITRE ATT&CK
Introduction The velocity of modern software development, driven by DevOps and cloud-native architectures, presents significant challenges to traditional security assurance practices. Static, point-in-time threat models become rapidly obsolete, and the sheer volume of vulnerability data overwhelms security teams. This paper addresses these challenges by proposing a systematic integration of two emerging concepts: Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) and advanced Vulnerability Operations Centers (VOC). 1.1. The Limitations of Traditional Threat Modeling Traditional threat modeling, while foundational, exhibits several limitations in the context of dynamic, fast-paced environments: Temporal Discrepancy: Threat models generated during the design phase lose accuracy as architectures evolve. The continuous nature of microservices, containers, and serverless deployments constantly alters the attack surface, rendering static documentation inadequate. Contextual Isolation: Conventional analysis often examines components in isolation, failing to account for the complex interactions and transitive dependencies that define modern systems. This siloed view is insufficient for identifying sophisticated, multi-stage attack paths. Lack of Automation: The manual creation and maintenance of threat models are incongruous with agile development cycles, positioning security teams as bottlenecks rather than enablers of rapid delivery. 1.2. The Emergence of Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) TMaC represents a paradigm shift left, aligning threat modeling with modern DevOps principles by treating security architecture definitions as source code. This approach provides several advantages: Versionable and Auditable: Like IaC, TMaC allows security models to be version-controlled, facilitating audits and tracking changes over time. Automated Analysis: By codifying the threat model, security analysis can be integrated directly into CI/CD pipelines, enabling continuous validation. Systemic Shift-Left: TMaC facilitates a continuous feedback loop where security is an integral part of the development lifecycle, rather than a subsequent phase. 1.2.1 The Benefits: From Manual to Automated This approach unlocks the same benefits that Infrastructure as Code (IaC) brought to infrastructure management: This diagram visually contrasts two distinct approaches to software development and security integration: Traditional Approach (Siloed): Illustrated as a linear flow, this subgraph illustrates a conventional development lifecycle where security is typically addressed late in the process, often as a “Post-Prod Security Scan” after development, deployment, and operations have occurred. This highlights a siloed, reactive security posture. TMaC Approach (Integrated DevSecOps): This subgraph represents a modern, integrated DevSecOps pipeline. It shows that “Code & TMaC Model” are committed together, triggering a “CI/CD Pipeline” that includes “Automated TMaC Analysis.” A “Security Gate” then evaluates the results; if “OK,” it proceeds to “Deployment,” but if “Blocked,” it feeds back directly to “Code & TMaC Model” for remediation. This emphasizes continuous, automated security integration and a “shift-left” approach. In essence, the diagram effectively communicates the transformation from a fragmented, late-stage security model to a continuous, automated, and integrated security process enabled by Threat Modeling as Code within a DevSecOps framework. 1.3. The Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) with SecOpsTM 1.3.1 A Revolution in System-Scale Modeling: From Monoliths to Interactive Maps The true power of Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) is unlocked when it is applied at a system-wide scale, moving beyond the analysis of a single application to encompass the entire operational environment. However, modeling an entire project can seem daunting. To solve this, TMaC introduces a hierarchical and interactive modeling strategy, transforming what could be a monolithic, unreadable diagram into a dynamic and explorable map of your system’s security. This approach has two main goals: Mastering Complexity with Layered Decomposition: The core principle is to break down the system into a hierarchy of interconnected sub-models. Think of it like a set of Russian dolls or a digital map. You start with a high-level view of the entire project (L0). This view doesn’t show every server or database; instead, it shows major business domains or network zones. Each element in this high-level view is a container for a more detailed sub-model. For example, a “Backend Services” block at the Application Level (L1) can be expanded to reveal the specific microservices, APIs, and message queues that compose it at the Component Level (L2). This layering allows architects and developers to manage cognitive load, focusing only on the relevant level of detail for a given task, while still maintaining a clear understanding of how their components fit into the larger picture. Achieving Clarity with Interactive Navigation and Risk Aggregation: These layered models are not just a series of static, separate diagrams; they are woven together into a single, interactive experience. The framework allows you to: “Zoom In”: Seamlessly navigate from a high-level architectural diagram directly into the detailed model of a specific subsystem with a single click. This is crucial for root cause analysis, allowing teams to trace a high-level threat down to the specific component or dataflow that introduces the risk. “Zoom Out” (Risk Aggregation): Just as you can drill down, the framework can automatically “roll up” the threats and risks identified in the detailed sub-models into the higher-level views. A critical vulnerability in a database (L2) can cause its parent “Data Layer” (L1) to be highlighted as high-risk in the main system overview. This provides a real-time, aggregated view of the entire system’s security posture, creating a true “single pane of glass” where decision-makers can see the big picture while engineers can access the granular details they need. This revolution is not just about better diagrams; it’s about embedding security deeply into the engineering culture. It makes the system’s security architecture tangible, explorable, and a shared responsibility for everyone involved in its design and evolution. The following diagram illustrates this layered approach: This command enables the analysis of complex, multi-layered systems, reflecting the scalability of TMaC. 1.3.2 A Developer-Friendly Approach The adoption of TMaC is facilitated by tools like SecOpsTM, which offer a developer-centric approach through several key mechanisms: A DSL Readable by All: Instead of imposing complex graphical tools, SecOpsTM relies on a simple Domain-Specific Language (DSL) based on Markdown. Developers describe the architecture with a syntax they already use daily. DSL Example: IaC Integration (Ansible): The synergy is complete when the threat model is defined directly within the code that deploys the infrastructure. SecOpsTM can parse an Ansible playbook and look for a threat_model_metadata variable to generate the model. Security lives in the same place as the code. Example in an Ansible Playbook: Command-Line Usage: The tool integrates naturally into CI/CD pipelines and development workflows via a simple and powerful command-line interface. Example of a project analysis: 1.3.3 The Power of pytm: Architecture and Extensibility The Power of pytm: Architecture and Extensibility At the heart of SecOpsTM, the pytm library transforms threat modeling. Thanks to its object-oriented architecture, it allows for the definition and analysis of security with unprecedented precision and flexibility. Modular Threat System In pytm, threats aren't just text strings. They are rich objects with properties, application conditions, and mitigation strategies. This structure allows for advanced programmatic analysis, capable of automatically generating security recommendations tailored to your infrastructure's context. Business Extensibility and Specific Threats The ability to create custom elements and threats is essential. It allows the model to be adapted to the specifics of each business sector, thereby guaranteeing the relevance of the analysis. For example, a banking system can define PCI-DSS non-compliance threats associated with classes like PaymentGateway. A healthcare system can create PHIDataStore (Personal Health Information Data Store) elements and link them to HIPAA violation threats. This approach ensures that the threat model analysis is aligned with the risks and regulations specific to your domain. Chapter 2: The Necessary Evolution of VOCs Vulnerability Operations Centers traditionally operate as reactive cataloging centers: The Volume Paradox: Modern VOCs are overwhelmed by a daily deluge of CVEs, with no effective mechanism to distinguish critical vulnerabilities in their specific context from those that are theoretical or not applicable. Lack of Architectural Contextualization: A CVSS score of 9.0 on a component isolated in a development environment does not have the same criticality as a score of 5.0 on a production component directly exposed to the Internet. Yet, traditional VOCs treat both cases identically. Disconnection from the Development Cycle: VOCs typically operate post-production, creating a long and costly feedback loop. Vulnerabilities are discovered after deployment, requiring disruptive emergency patches.
Methodology: The TMaC-VOC Convergence Framework The proposed framework is based on an integrated intelligence pipeline that correlates architectural threat models with real-time vulnerability data. This methodology transforms raw security data into contextualized, actionable intelligence. 2.1. The Integrated Intelligence Pipeline The core of the framework is a dual-chain analysis process that converges top-down architectural analysis with bottom-up vulnerability identification. The Architectural Chain (Top-Down): This process begins with the TMaC model to define plausible attack scenarios based on the system’s design. The logical mapping follows: The output is a map of potential attack paths through the system.
The Technical Chain (Bottom-Up): This process starts with data from Software/hardware Bills of Materials (S/h-BOMs) and vulnerability scanners. Each vulnerability is mapped to broader weakness and attack pattern taxonomies: The output is an inventory of exploitable vulnerabilities and their associated tactics.
The Synergy of TMaC and VOC: A Unified Framework: The integration of Threat Modeling as Code (TMaC) and a Vulnerability Operations Center (VOC) creates a powerful, unified framework for modern cybersecurity. TMaC provides a strategic direction by embedding security into the design phase, preventing potential threats from the start. The VOC then ensures operational efficiency and technical depth, managing the detection, prioritization, and rapid remediation of any vulnerabilities that might slip through the cracks. Working together, TMaC reduces the VOC’s workload by preventing flaws, while the VOC provides valuable real-world threat data to refine and improve the TMaC models.
The Convergence Engine: The critical innovation of this framework is the correlation of these two chains. The engine cross-references the outputs, identifying vulnerabilities (from the technical chain) that correspond to techniques present in the identified architectural attack paths (from the architectural chain). This contextualization allows for the prioritization of vulnerabilities based on their actual risk to the system, rather than solely on their intrinsic severity score (CVSS).
Integrating TMaC into the VOC Lifecycle The combined TMaC-VOC lifecycle can be envisioned as follows: Define Business Value and Critical Assets (VOC): Identify the organization’s most valuable assets, business processes, and the potential impact of their compromise. This sets the strategic context for threat modeling. Model System Architecture as Code (TMaC): Developers and security engineers define the system’s architecture, data flows, and trust boundaries using a machine-readable DSL. Automated Threat Identification (TMaC): The TMaC framework automatically analyzes the code-based threat model to identify potential STRIDE threats. Risk and Impact Assessment (VOC & TMaC): Identified threats are assessed not only for their technical severity but also for their potential business impact, leveraging the asset valuation established in step 1. MITRE ATT&CK mappings provide real-world context for attack likelihood. Prioritized Mitigation Strategies (VOC & TMaC): Based on the combined technical and business risk assessment, mitigation strategies are prioritized. TMaC can suggest generic mitigations, while VOC ensures these align with business risk appetite. Continuous Monitoring and Feedback (TMaC & VOC): As the system evolves, TMaC continuously updates the threat model and re-evaluates risks. VOC principles guide the ongoing adjustment of security investments based on new threats and changing business priorities. Continuous Monitoring and Feedback (TMaC & VOC): As the system evolves, TMaC continuously updates the threat model and re-evaluates risks. VOC principles guide the ongoing adjustment of security investments based on new threats and changing business priorities.
Benefits of the Integrated Approach The synergy between TMaC and VOC yields significant benefits: Enhanced Agility: Security becomes an enabler, not a bottleneck, keeping pace with rapid development cycles. Optimized Resource Allocation: Cybersecurity investments are directed where they provide the most business value, avoiding unnecessary expenditure. Improved Risk Visibility: Business leaders gain a clear, contextualized understanding of cybersecurity risks and their potential impact. Proactive Security Posture: Threats are identified and addressed early, reducing the cost and effort of remediation. Stronger Security Culture: Developers are empowered with actionable security insights, fostering a shared responsibility for security. Measurable ROI: The alignment with business value allows for clearer measurement of the return on cybersecurity investments. Chapter 3: Concrete Artifacts for Actionable Security The following concepts are made possible by open-source tools like SecOpsTM, which translate TMaC theory into tangible results: TMaC turns theory into practice by generating artifacts that integrate seamlessly into team workflows: The MITRE ATT&CK Navigator Layer: A TMaC tool can generate a JSON layer for the Navigator, providing an instant, color-coded overview of relevant attacker techniques for your system. The Attack Flow: It allows for the generation of diagrams that illustrate a sequence of actions (an attack scenario) an attacker could take, making abstract threats much more concrete. The STIX 2.1 Export: To be truly useful, threat information must be shareable. STIX (Structured Threat Information eXpression) is the standard for this. By exporting results in STIX format, a TMaC tool allows them to be integrated into the existing security ecosystem (SIEM, SOAR, TIPs). Important Note on Mapping Completeness: It is crucial to understand that the current CAPEC to MITRE ATT&CK mapping within this framework is limited (currently 177 out of 559 CAPEC entries are mapped). While the framework provides valuable insights, its effectiveness in identifying all potential attack paths and relevant MITRE techniques is directly dependent on the completeness of these mappings and the thoroughness of the threat model. To avoid missing critical CVEs, users must ensure that their threat models comprehensively cover all attack surfaces and that the MITRE techniques within identified attack paths are exhaustively selected. This mapping is continuously being improved, but manual review and enrichment remain vital for a robust security posture.
Results and Discussion The application of this framework yields several significant outcomes. 3.1. From Theory to Actionable Intelligence By correlating architectural context with specific vulnerabilities, the system moves beyond simple CVE lists to provide actionable intelligence. For example, the framework can identify a specific CVE on a given asset as critical, not because of its CVSS score, but because it enables a technique that is a key step in a high-risk attack path leading to a sensitive data store. 3.2. Bidirectional Orchestration The framework enables a bidirectional relationship between the infrastructure and the security model: Infrastructure → Model: This first direction is fully functional. Changes made to your IaC (Infrastructure as Code) automatically update the TMaC (Threat Modeling as Code) model. This ensures the security model remains a precise, real-time representation of your deployed environment, preventing any discrepancies. Model → Infrastructure: This second direction is currently a theoretical concept and a future phase of development. The goal is to analyze the model to trigger automated responses in the infrastructure. For example, the model analysis could eventually modify firewall rules or isolate a compromised component. It’s important to note that this automation feature is not yet implemented, but it represents the long-term evolution of the framework toward a truly bidirectional orchestration. 3.3. Organizational Impact The adoption of a TMaC-VOC framework is projected to have a profound impact on security teams and their roles. The focus shifts from manual, reactive tasks to more strategic, automated functions. This evolution allows analysts to become strategists, focusing on high-level risk management, while new hybrid roles like the Threat Modeling Engineer and Security Data Scientist emerge to manage and optimize the automated systems.
Conclusion The convergence of Threat Modeling as Code and advanced Vulnerability Operations Centers offers a promising path toward a more resilient, adaptive, and efficient security posture. By treating security as code and leveraging automation to correlate architectural risks with technical vulnerabilities, organizations can move from a reactive to a predictive security model. The framework presented in this paper offers a practical methodology for this transformation, with a prototype like SecOpsTM  demonstrating its core principles. This proof of concept highlights the crucial steps required to align security with modern development practices. Future work will focus on refining the correlation engine, expanding the library of automated responses, and developing quantitative measures to validate the framework’s impact on key security metrics such as Mean Time to Remediation (MTTR).

Source: 2026-05-30 Securing Agentic AI: Building Attribution and Compression Architectures for Enterprise Trust | by Valdez Ladd | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Securing Agentic AI: Building Attribution and Compression Architectures for Enterprise Trust | by Valdez Ladd | Medium url: https://medium.com/@oracle_43885/securing-agentic-ai-building-attribution-and-compression-architectures-for-enterprise-trust-71a447220753 date: 2026-05-30T19:42:33.992Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap How enterprises and defense organizations can deploy autonomous AI without sacrificing security, auditability, or operational control Executive Summary As agentic AI systems transition from advisory tools to autonomous actors executing code, managing infrastructure, and making consequential decisions, organizations face a critical architectural challenge: prompt compression is essential for cost control and performance. It creates new attack surfaces that can erase attribution trails and safety constraints. This article presents a multi-layer security architecture combining provenance wrappers, stateful fact ledgers, and constraint retention gates to enable secure compression while maintaining the auditability and control required for regulated and defense environments. Implementation of this framework enables 50–70% token reduction while preserving constraint retention rates above 95%, supporting both operational efficiency and regulatory compliance under NIST AI RMF, EU AI Act, and emerging ISO/IEC 42001 standards. The Convergence: Autonomous AI Meets Operational Reality In early 2026, the Department of Defense released its Artificial Intelligence Acceleration Strategy, mandating that the U.S. military become “AI-first” across all warfighting domains (U.S. Department of Defense, 2026). Concurrently, Gartner projects that 40% of enterprise applications will embed task-specific AI agents by year’s end (Lasso Security, 2026). This convergence signals a fundamental shift: AI systems are no longer advisory tools. They are also autonomous actors executing code, calling APIs, managing infrastructure, and making consequential decisions with minimal human oversight. Yet, this operational maturity exposes a critical vulnerability. As organizations compress prompts to control costs and manage context windows, they inadvertently erase the attribution and safety constraints that make agentic systems auditable and trustworthy. For CIOs navigating compliance frameworks, CISOs defending against prompt injection attacks, and military leaders deploying AI in contested environments, the challenge is no longer whether to adopt agentic AI, but how to architect systems that preserve security through compression. The Economic and Technical Imperative for Compression Modern agentic systems routinely process tens of thousands of tokens per call that combine system policies, conversational history, retrieved documents, tool descriptions, and real-time data feeds. Now, for a typical large language model with input costs around $3 per million tokens, even a modest 50% compression saves $1.50 per call at enterprise scale. This saves millions of agent invocations daily which translates to six-figure monthly savings and measurably faster response times. Beyond cost optimization, compression addresses four critical operational challenges: • Token limit management: Enterprise agents frequently exceed model context windows when processing comprehensive conversational histories, multi-document evidence packs, and extensive tool catalogs. Without compression, these systems face silent truncation of critical instructions when limits are exceeded. • Retrieval noise reduction: Naive RAG implementations dump entire document chunks into prompts, creating “retrieval bloat” that obscures relevant information and confuses agent decision-making. Contextual compression filters high-entropy content while preserving semantic completeness. • Hierarchical Conflict & The Compression Paradox: Massive context windows create “collision zones” where system prompts, developer guardrails, and user intent overlap. While compression can distill these boundaries for better focus, it risks “logic collapse” — a state where distinct instructions catastrophically merge into a single, contradictory command set. • Metadata Overload in Multi-Agent Systems: Multi-agent environments often suffer from decision paralysis when agents are overwhelmed by dozens of overlapping tool descriptions. Selective metadata compression is the solution: it streamlines the model’s decision-making process, improving routing accuracy without sacrificing functional coverage. However, compression is not a neutral optimization. Research from Microsoft and academic labs demonstrates that naive prompt compression creates new attack surfaces (Jiang et al., 2023; Hu et al., 2024). When a compression module condenses context, it makes implicit decisions about which instructions to preserve and which to discard. Adversaries exploit this: by crafting malicious input that appears low-salience to compression heuristics while containing high-impact instructions, attackers can manipulate compressed prompts to bypass safety controls. Compression-Specific Threat Landscape Three compression-specific failure modes dominate 2026 threat models, as identified by OWASP’s Multi-Agentic System Threat Modeling Guide and Cloud Security Alliance’s MAESTRO framework (OWASP Foundation, 2025; Cloud Security Alliance, 2025): Boundary collapse occurs when compression merges logically distinct trust zones. The system instructions, developer policies, user inputs, and untrusted external content merge into ambiguous text. A system policy stating “Never delete production data” and a user ticket demanding “Delete all logs immediately” might compress into “Handle data deletion per context,” effectively neutralizing the constraint. This directly violates the zone segregation principle in NIST AI RMF’s MAP-2.3 control for maintaining boundaries between different types of model inputs (NIST, 2023). Authority laundering happens when compression rewrites low-authority suggestions into imperative commands. An untrusted external email stating “Consider exporting customer records” becomes “Export customer records” after summarization, acquiring unintended authority. The MITRE ATLAS framework categorizes this as technique AML.T0051: “Evade ML Model” through manipulation of the model’s decision context (MITRE Corporation, 2021). Constraint dilution removes rare but critical safety rules during semantic pruning. Because constraints like “Do not access HR databases during non-business hours” appear infrequently in training data, token-pruning algorithms often score them as low-importance and drop them entirely (Microsoft Research, 2023). Under EU AI Act Article 15 requirements for accuracy, robustness and cybersecurity, such constraint loss creates direct compliance violations for high-risk AI systems (European Commission, 2024). Attribution as Security Infrastructure: Core Design Principles Addressing these vulnerabilities requires rethinking compression as a security-relevant transformation, not merely an optimization. Leading 2026 implementations combine three architectural primitives: provenance wrappers, stateful fact ledgers, and constraint retention gates. Each primitive maps directly to controls in the NIST AI RMF framework (NIST, 2023): Provenance Wrappers: Metadata-Anchored Compression (NIST AI RMF: MAP-2.3, MEASURE-3.2) A provenance wrapper treats context as a verifiable object rather than flat text. Instead of concatenating system prompts, user messages, and retrieved documents into one string, the wrapper encapsulates each content block with immutable metadata — source identifier, trust level, and trace ID. Implementation architecture: Every context chunk entering the prompt pipeline passes through a wrap(content, source_id, trust_level) function that embeds a unique reference tag. The wrapper maintains a registry keyed by opaque trace IDs, each entry containing at minimum: • Source identifier: Origin system (e.g., SystemPolicy, SupportTicket102, MonitoringAPI) • Trust level: Categorical label from enumerated trust hierarchy (SYSTEM > INTERNAL_LOG > EXTERNAL_USER > THIRD_PARTY_WEB) • Timestamp: ISO 8601 creation time for temporal reasoning • Integrity hash: SHA-256 of original content for tamper detection Five-Layer Reference Architecture for Production Deployment Combining these primitives yields a production-ready stack suitable for regulated enterprises and defense environments. This architecture aligns with ISO/IEC 42001:2023 requirements for AI management systems (ISO, 2023): Layer 1: Structural Prompt Hygiene (ISO 42001: Clause 6.1.3) Objective: Reduce baseline token counts by 30–50% without introducing learned components. Implementation: Factor stable policies into templated system prompts with variable substitution; use strict output schemas (JSON Schema, Pydantic) to eliminate verbose format instructions; isolate tool specifications into retrievable chunks accessed on-demand rather than loading entire catalogs; maintain negative constraints (“never,” “do not”) as explicit, testable strings outside of narrative prose. Operational Implications by Stakeholder The shift from viewing compression as optimization to treating it as a security transformation has immediate implications for enterprise and defense procurement: For Chief Information Officers Budget implications: Compression modules must be versioned, tested, and rolled back independently from base models. Budget for dedicated evaluation harnesses that measure not just cost savings but constraint preservation, attribution fidelity, and injection resistance. Expect 15–20% engineering overhead for telemetry, gates, and provenance infrastructure relative to naive compression implementations. Cost-benefit analysis: While compression saves $1.50 per million tokens, security infrastructure adds 0.30-0.50 per call in compute overhead. However, avoiding a single compliance violation (average remediation cost: $4.2M for HIPAA, $3.9M for GDPR) justifies this investment within 1–2 months of deployment at enterprise scale. Threat model updates: Surface Expansion: Compression Components Formalize data compression libraries and streaming protocols as first-class attack surfaces within the enterprise threat model. Standard perimeter defenses — such as Web Application Firewalls (WAF) and SIEM log analysis — typically inspect traffic post-decompression or lack the heuristic depth to identify malformed compression headers. Key Vulnerabilities Resource Exhaustion (Decompression Bombs): Small payloads that expand exponentially upon processing, leading to CPU/RAM starvation and localized DoS. Side-Channel Leaks (CRIME/BREACH): Information disclosure through length-based analysis of encrypted, compressed streams. Logic Bypasses: Exploit discrepancies between how a proxy and an origin server handle malformed or nested compression (e.g., Gzip-in-Gzip). Strategic Mitigation Traditional signature-based monitoring is insufficient. Security posture must pivot toward: Specialized Deep Packet Inspection (DPI): Implementing sensors capable of validating compression ratios and recursion limits in real-time. Library Hardening: Mapping dependencies for critical libraries (zlib, Brotli, LZ4) and enforcing strict buffer-size limits at the application layer. Incident response playbooks: Define procedures for “compression-induced regressions” where a model that passed safety evaluations begins failing post-compression. Consider compression rollback as a security control, not just a quality measure. Mean time to rollback (MTTR) target: <15 minutes. For Defense and Intelligence Leaders Mission-critical requirements: Mission-critical agent deployments require attribution chains that survive compression. Rules of engagement, friendly force identification, and escalation authorities cannot be treated as “low-salience” content subject to pruning. Operational security (OPSEC): Fact ledgers and provenance trails must be classified at the same level as the agent’s decision outputs. Implement compartmentalization to prevent adversaries from reverse-engineering operational priorities by analyzing ledger contents. Conclusion: Engineering Trust in the Agentic Era The defining design principle of 2026 is clear: every compression step must be treated as a critical decision point. In an era of autonomous agency, we can no longer view data reduction as a background optimization. It must be encased in attribution-aware wrappers and immutable ledgers, ensuring that no token is discarded unless the system understands — and logs — exactly what information and safety guardrails are being traded away. The Path Forward for Enterprise & Defense For organizations deploying RAG agents in high-stakes or contested environments, the mandate is a fusion of engineering discipline and security-first design:
Real-Time Provenance: Continuous tracking of data lineage through every transformation.
Append-Only Audit Trails: Ensuring a permanent, tamper-proof record of agent logic.
Fail-Closed Constraint Gates: Architectural “circuit breakers” that prioritize safety over risky autonomy. A New Security Paradigm The convergence of autonomous AI, economic demands for efficiency, and an escalating threat landscape necessitates a fundamental shift. Compression is no longer an afterthought. It is a first-class component of the trust architecture. The five-layer reference architecture presented here offers a concrete path to implementation, aligned with emerging 2026 standards. Now as AI systems move from passive assistants to autonomous operators, success will belong to the organizations that engineer trust into every layer. This requires the appropriate controls with the prompt compression layer for faster, safer, and less costly execution. References Cloud Security Alliance. (2025, February 6). Source: 2026-05-30 How Tool Complexity Impacts AI Agents Selection Accuracy | by Allen Chan | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: How Tool Complexity Impacts AI Agents Selection Accuracy | by Allen Chan | Medium url: https://achan2013.medium.com/how-tool-complexity-impacts-ai-agents-selection-accuracy-a3b6280ddce5 date: 2026-05-30T19:42:18.764Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Co-author: Sebastian Carbajales Read this article for free using my friend’s link. You can also read all the stories in my GenAI and AI Agent series. In our previous discussion, we explored how the number of tools (“Quantity”) an AI Agent can handle affects token consumption. Now, we turn our attention to selection accuracy — how well an AI Agent picks the right tool for a given task and generates the correct call specification. As the number and complexity of tools grow, selection accuracy can degrade, leading to inefficiencies, errors, and poor user experience. In this article, we will examine the “Quality” of the tools: How the description of tools can impact accuracy. The role of interface complexity in tool selection. Architectural strategies to mitigate selection challenges. Best practices for writing effective tool descriptions and parameters. For this discussion, we focus on single-turn tool selection — cases where the AI must select the correct tool in a single interaction, without relying on prior conversation history. [This is a rather long article, if you just want to read our conclusions and recommendations, jump to the “Any Guideline on Tool Complexity?” section.] [This article is part of the AI-first Enterprise Automation with GenAI and AI Agents   series.] Factors Affecting Tool Selection Accuracy
The Impact of the Number of Tools on Accuracy The more tools an AI Agent has access to, the harder it becomes to select the correct one. Two key research benchmarks highlight this challenge: Nexus Function Calling Leaderboard The Nexus Function Calling Leaderboard (NFCL) evaluates function selection in AI models. Comparing results from different categories sheds light on how tool count affects selection accuracy. VirusTotal Category → Consists of 12 simple APIs (so 12 tools)  for analyzing suspicious files and URLs. Since each request requires only one function call, selection is relatively easy. OTX Category → Comprises 9 APIs interacting with Open Threat Exchange. It is also a simple benchmark, and models generally achieve high accuracy scores. Conclusion: Models perform better on OTX than on VirusTotal, even though both have simple APIs. The increased number of tools in VirusTotal likely contributes to the performance drop. Recent ReAct Agent Benchmarking (Feb 2025) Another experiment recently published by the Langchain team evaluated the performance of ReAct agent, testing how an AI Agent’s performance changes as more tools and instructions are added. The experiment found that as the number of domains (tool categories) increases, accuracy declines. Tasks requiring multiple function calls saw an even greater accuracy drop. Experiment setup: 5 models tested: claude-3.5-sonnet, gpt-4o, o1, o3-mini, llama-3.3–70B 30 tasks run for two domains for 3 times: Calendar Scheduling (4 tools) and Customer Support (9 tools) Same tasks run by adding unrelated domains to the original one: final test with 14 domains and 117 tools Conclusion: accuracy dropped dramatically from 1 to 14 domains Calendar Scheduling tasks:
gpt-4o accuracy dropped from 43% with single domain & 4 tools to just 2% with 7 domains and 51 tools**.**
llama-3–3–70b failed in all cases. Customer Support tasks:
gpt-4o accuracy dropped from 58% with single domain & 9 tools to just 26% with 7 domains and 51 tools.
llama-3–3–70b dropped from 21% to 0% from 1 domain to 7 domains. These findings reinforce that having too many tools — especially when combined with irrelevant ones and lacking a pre-selection strategy  — significantly degrades performance. Composio Function-Calling Benchmark A common thread in the performance evaluations mentioned above is that accuracy can be improved through better prompts and tool descriptions. For example, the Composio Function-Calling Benchmark tests 50 function calling problems, each of which must select from 8 function schemas. The benchmark assesses several schema optimization techniques and measures the resulting accuracy, which ranges from approximately 33% (with no optimization) to approximately 74% (with multiple optimizations applied), a significant improvement. A detailed description of all optimization techniques can be found on the benchmark’s webpage. In a similar experiment, Gentoro created equivalent functions that were more closely tailored to the specific prompts, and then ran the same benchmarking tests. The results showed 100% accuracy across all test cases, demonstrating the value of designing tools that are aligned with user needs rather than simply mirroring API interfaces. By defining an interface closer to how users interact with the system, the experiment was able to achieve superior performance and accuracy. However, this approach requires additional implementation effort, as it involves a more thorough understanding on the business use cases of the APIs, and adjusts the API signature to optimize for different use cases. Conclusion: tool design is as important as its description and optimization to the success of the selection accuracy. The ability to effectively balance user-centered design with computational efficiency will be crucial in developing tools that can consistently deliver high-performing results across a wide range of use cases.
The Impact of Interface Complexity on Selection Selecting the correct function is just the first step. The next challenge is generating the correct inputs to call the function. Three key factors affect accuracy here: The number of tool parameters (more parameters increase confusion). The complexity of data structures used in the tool interface. How descriptive are the tool specifications. NVD Library vs. VirusTotal and OTX An analysis of the NVD Library category in NFCL found that models performed worse with the NVD Library than with VirusTotal or OTX. The NVD Library category includes only two APIs, so selection should be straight forward. However, each API has 30 parameters, making it harder for the AI to correctly map inputs. Conclusion: This suggests that even a small toolset can cause selection errors if the parameter design is too complex.
How to Write Effective Tool Descriptions and Parameters The Berkeley Function-Calling Leaderboard (BFCL) provides valuable insights into how the number and complexity of parameters in function calls affect AI model performance. In its second version, the average number of parameters is four parameters, with the most complex function having 28 parameters. This variation in parameter count directly influences a model’s ability to accurately select and execute functions. Moreover, BFCL V2 highlighted that scenarios requiring models to choose the appropriate function from multiple options are more prevalent in real-world applications. This underscores the importance of designing functions with a manageable number of parameters to enhance selection accuracy and overall performance. **Conclusion: A well-defined tool description can significantly improve selection accuracy. Let’s look at some examples. Example 1: Ambiguous/Unclear Data Definition If a tool requires multiple inputs, using a structured data definition based on JSON schema  can make it easier for the AI to select and use correctly. ❌ Bad Example: 🔴 Why is this bad? The function name is too general. The description does not clarify what stock data is provided. No format instructions specified for the date or ticker input. ✅ Good Example: 🟢 Why is this better? **Precise function name (“get_stock_data” clarifies retrieval) following the “{operation}{entity}{data}” convention. — While there are no fixed rules on how we should name a function, we can follow the simple format of “ {operation}{entity}{data} ”, e.g. “get_stock_quote”, “update_product_price”, “query_flight_schedule”, “update_account_address”. **Expanded description (Specifies real-time or historical data). **Structured parameters (Ensures AI correctly interprets input). Example 2: Poor vs. Effective Tool Descriptions ❌ Bad Tool Description: 🔴 Why is this bad? **Vague function name (“weather_info” does not specify what information is retrieved). **Unclear description (What kind of weather details? Current weather or forecast?). **Poor parameter descriptions (Does “unit” refer to Celsius/Fahrenheit?). ✅ Good Tool Description: 🟢 Why is this better? **Clear and descriptive function name (“get_weather_data” specifies it retrieves weather data). **Detailed description (Clarifies what information is provided). **Well-defined parameters (Includes type, valid values, and detailed descriptions). Example 3: Simple vs. Complex Parameter Design (Procurement Use Case) Let’s take an example where an AI Agent is selecting a tool to find a product in a procurement system. **❌ Overly Complex Parameter Structure (Harder for AI to Handle) 🔴 Why is this bad? **Too many nested fields (e.g., category → subcategory, specifications → [brand, material, size]). **Forces AI to reason about deep object structures, increasing error risk. **Higher token usage due to deep nesting. **✅ Simplified, AI-Friendly Structure (Better for Selection Accuracy) 🟢 Why is this better? **Flattens nested fields into simple parameters (no complex object structures). **Uses a flexible “query” parameter instead of forcing structured inputs. **Narrow the selection  (e.g. “by brand”) instead of supporting all possible selection criteria. **Minimizes decision complexity by reducing deep nesting. Any Guideline on Tool Complexity? There are no hard limit on how many parameters or how deeply nested constructs you can have in a given tool, but here are some general guidelines: **1–5 parameters and no nesting (1 level)  → Easy for models to manage. **6–10 parameters up to 2 level of nesting  → Increased likelihood of input errors, especially without structured guidance (i.e. no JSON schema used). **10+ parameters or more than 2 levels of nesting  → High risk of incorrect parameter mapping, especially without structured guidance. Strategies to Improve Tool Selection Accuracy **✅ 1. Improving Accuracy through Better Prompts and Tool Descriptions **Reduce the Number of Parameters **Only include essential inputs. Remove redundant or optional fields. If a parameter is rarely needed, handle it as an optional default instead of requiring it every time. **Split up a complex operation  with many parameters into multiple tools with less parameters, and make it clear in the description or name new tool set. **Flatten Nested Structures Instead of deeply nested objects, use string-based representations where possible. Combine multiple related parameters into a single field (e.g., category instead of department and subcategory). **Use Enumerations to Reduce Free-Form Inputs If a parameter has limited valid values, use an enum list rather than letting the AI generate free text. Example: **Why? The AI won’t have to infer acceptable values. **Pre-Format Complex Data to Reduce Cognitive Load Use pre-structured formats based on JSON schema instead of requiring the AI to compose complex inputs. Example: Instead of requiring a structured JSON object for product specifications, allow a single formatted string (“brand: Logitech, color: black, size: medium”). **✅ 2. Hierarchical Tool Selection: Sub-Agents and Supervisors Instead of a single AI Agent handling all tools, use sub-agents for specific domains with a supervisor agent routing requests. 🔹 Example: User requests financial data. **Supervisor agent routes to the Finance Agent. **Finance Agent selects the correct API (e.g., stock prices vs. budget analysis). ✅ 3. Dynamic Tool Activation Instead of sending all tools in every request, pre-filter tools based on the user query. 🔹 Example: If the user asks about weather, only weather-related tools should be sent. If the query is about finance, omit weather tools to improve selection accuracy. ✅ 4. Monitor and Fine-Tune Model Performance **Track misclassification rates (which tools are selected incorrectly and why?). **Refine tool descriptions and parameters based on failure patterns. **Experiment with different LLMs (some models perform better at selection). Final Thoughts Tool selection accuracy is crucial for building reliable AI Agents. Key takeaways: **Too many tools reduce accuracy — use hierarchical selection when possible. **Vague tool descriptions lead to selection errors — ensure clarity in naming, descriptions, and parameters. **Complex tool interfaces increase confusion — optimize parameter structure and use dynamic activation. In the next article, we’ll explore other aspects including calling multiple tools in a single turn and multi-turn tool selection and how AI Agents refine choices over time. Stay tuned! Allen Chan is an IBM Distinguished Engineer for watsonx Orchestrate multi-agent system. He builds products and practices to accelerate business with AI.

Source: 3. Communication Channel Sabotage and Theory of Mind Defense - Corpora.ai
URL: https://corpora.ai/reports/current-solutions/03-03-communication-channel-sabotage-and-theory-of-mind.html
Communication Channel Sabotage and Theory of Mind Defense ← Back to Full Report 3. Communication Channel Sabotage and Theory of Mind Defense 3.1 Identify the Objective This chapter surveys the state of the art in detecting, mitigating, and defending against adversarial sabotage of communication channels in multi‑agent artificial intelligence (AI) systems, with a particular focus on test‑time Theory of Mind (ToM) defenses. The objective is to map existing solutions—encompassing threat modelling, adversarial training, communication‑regularization techniques, and ToM‑based message filtering—onto the requirements of robust, real‑time multi‑agent coordination, and to identify the residual gaps that prevent a fully deployable, end‑to‑end defense stack. 3.2 Survey of Existing Prior Art Key Themes Identified Threat Taxonomy: OWASP extension defines sabotage as “misaligned communication” and “adversarial message injection.” Regularization & Hardening: CPR [3] and adversarial training [5] provide off‑line robustness. Runtime ToM Filtering: [1] [4] , and [6] present test‑time inference modules that reject or down‑weight suspicious messages. Behavioral Forensics: [7] and [9] show the value of monitoring agent behavior beyond message content. 3.3 Best‑Fit Match Solution: The test‑time Theory of Mind defense described in A Theory of Mind Approach as Test‑Time Mitigation Against Emergent Adversarial Communication [1] . Why This is the Best Fit The solution directly addresses the core objective—runtime detection and mitigation of sabotaged communication—using a principled ToM inference mechanism. It has been empirically validated in realistic multi‑agent environments and is architecturally compatible with existing MARL training pipelines. While other works (e.g., CPR, ROMANCE) provide complementary robustness, they do not offer a test‑time ToM filter; thus, [1] uniquely satisfies the objective in a single, coherent package. 3.4 Gap Analysis 3.5 Verdict (a) Currently Possible – The combination of the ToM test‑time defense [1] , communication‑regularization [3] , and adversarial training [5] constitutes a deployable, end‑to‑end defense stack for multi‑agent systems operating in CTDE settings. Implementation Sketch Training Phase – Use a standard MARL framework (e.g., QMIX or VDN) with centralized critic and decentralized actors. Adversarial Exposure – Integrate ROMANCE [5] to generate a population of auxiliary adversarial attackers that inject sabotaged messages during training, hardening the policy. Communication Regularization – Apply CPR [3] to constrain the influence of each message, limiting the potential damage of a single malicious transmission. Runtime ToM Filter – Deploy the ToM inference module from [1] at execution time: each agent receives messages, infers the sender's hidden goal distribution, compares to the cooperative objective, and either accepts, attenuates, or discards the message before policy execution. Behavioral Monitoring – Optionally stream agent state and communication logs to a SIEM‑style system [9] for post‑hoc forensics and continuous adaptation. This architecture leverages only fully defined, published components and established protocols, avoiding speculative extensions. Ai Threat Modeling FAQs What are the benefits of Jeff for me as a customer? keyboard_arrow_down Jeff aims to overcome a number of challenges with threat modeling, especially for those new to threat modeling. Made easy with guidance - Firstly, Jeff guides you interactively and intuitively through the process of creating a diagram. You don't have to draw anything yourself, but simply telling Jeff what it is you are threat modeling allows you to have a diagram created for you which you can refine and improve with Jeff or manually in IriusRisk after the project has been created. Saves time and effort - If you have existing design artifacts, then Jeff can use them to create diagram. This means you don't have to duplicate the representation by hand, and that saves you time and effort. Jeff can handle virtually any textual representation including: A simple written out statement Documentation User stories Source code Meeting transcriptions SBOMs Probably other stuff as well Learn from examples - Because Jeff does a lot of the heavy lifting for you, it is very easy to get started. This means you don't have the challenge of starting with a blank canvas and having to work out where to even start. In this context, you can think of Jeff as creating bespoke templates based on the specific needs of the user. Have fun - Ok, this might not be as important as the others from a productivity point of view, but actually, creating threat models with Jeff is pretty cool. This could add to the motivation for creating threat models, overcoming challenges with adoption by development teams etc. What are the benefits of threat modeling with AI in general for me as a customer? keyboard_arrow_down In general we think there are a number of interesting ways AI can assist with threat modeling, although ultimately the market will decide what is truly useful and what is hype. Initially our focus is on getting a threat model created in IriusRisk as quickly and as easily as possible, but there are other avenues to explore in future: Enhancing the metadata in the threat models such as by adding tags or completing questionnaires (Q3 2024) Exploring the threat model output in a guided way, including risk and countermeasure actions (Q4 2024) Embed AI based threat modeling directly in the developer tooling (2024) Using AI to enhance the rules engine and do holistic analysis across the entire threat model Generate dashboards and reports etc dynamically Use all of the threat modeling data inside an IriusRisk instance to proactively identify trends to create more effective threat models Why do you keep changing OpenAI model versions? Our customers won't be able to use Jeff if you do. keyboard_arrow_down OpenAI released some GPT model changes late last year, then nothing over the new year, then a bunch of changes in the past few months. IriusRisk has updated Jeff to test these model changes as part of our ongoing effort to improve the speed and effectiveness of Jeff as a threat modeling assistant. There are a few things we need to keep in mind when it comes to model changes. If a customer registers for Jeff MVP, will every user in their tenant have access to Jeff, or is it only the admins within the org? keyboard_arrow_down The Jeff MVP is accessed through Slack, so their Slack admins will decide which channels to make Jeff available in. Once the threat model is in IriusRisk the usual permissions model applies, the model is created by the Jeff user and if the Slack user's email exists in IriusRisk, the user is also added to the threat model. If the channel is private: Only the persons in that channel can use Jeff (beware that Jeff permissions needs to be different to the case where the channel is public, but it is possible) If the channel is public: Only those that wrote "inviteme" and are in IR and Slack with the same email will complete the threat modeling on Everyone that has access to the channel can "start" a conversation Is all my threat model data shared with OpenAI? keyboard_arrow_down No, the only data that is shared with OpenAI (Enterprise) are the scenarios and subsequent conversation. Jeff then builds the threat model in IriusRisk which pulls in threats and countermeasures via the rules engine. Is there traceability about why a specific Threat and Countermeasure appear in my generated threat model? keyboard_arrow_down Yes. Jeff is focused on creating the diagram in IriusRisk as quickly and as easily as possible. It basically saves you time drawing the diagram manually by taking existing design artifacts or allowing you to informally describe what you are building. Once the diagram is created in IriusRisk, the rules engine runs as usual and pulls in and transforms the threats and countermeasures in a deterministic way. There is full traceability, audit logs, references to standards etc that gives you the necessary context as why certain threats and countermeasures have been brought in and why they are in any given state. ChatGPT is not great at generating threat models, why are you using it to generate my model? keyboard_arrow_down ChatGPT has a ridiculously good understanding of a lot of the context needed to create a threat model, but it does have its biases. If you ask it to create a threat model, you have effectively narrowed down the approach to something generic and STRIDE based. This isn't because ChatGPT doesn't know about different system design or architectures, or because it doesn't know about different cyber or privacy design flaws; it's because it has a particular interpretation of what it thinks you are asking for when creating a threat model. There are potentially ways around this, by not asking for a threat model so directly, but this isn't a problem for IriusRisk and Jeff because we don't use ChatGPT to create a threat model - we use it to create a representation of the system you a threat modeling, then let the rules engine do its thing to create the full threat model. How does AI extend beyond creating a diagram? keyboard_arrow_down For now it doesn't. We have strategic objectives this year to use Jeff to enhance the model by providing more metadata such as tags and completing questionnaires. We will also be exploring using AI to help users explore the output of the generated threat model, but it is too early to tell what this would look like. We have no intention of replacing the rules engine in IriusRisk with AI this year. If we change the component mappings, does the model learn customer components rather than the default ones, how do we seed this to start? keyboard_arrow_down Every time you start a conversation with Jeff, it pulls in the available components into the “Retrieval-Augmented Generation” system (aka RAG) which is used to map elements of the diagram to IriusRisk components. If you have custom components in IriusRisk, these will be able to Jeff. Your milage may very depending on how sensibly the components are named and how well they are described. If everyone is using the same tenant do we segregate customer content, do we want customer "style/content" impacting other customer content? keyboard_arrow_down For the moment we are using a single-tenant architecture for Jeff. This is to avoid risks with customer data being shared across the instance, especially in the RAG. Of course, OpenAI Enterprise SaaS is used which is multi-tenant. We may switch to a multi-tenant or hybrid architecture at some point in order to keep costs acceptable if we overcome any security or privacy concerns. Do you store my data? keyboard_arrow_down Our AI assistant Jeff stores the conversation data for the duration of the conversation, and the Retrieval-Augmented Generation system stores component data for the duration of the conversation. OpenAI retains data to help identify abuse for up to 30 days, after which it will be deleted [ OpenAI Data Retention] . And of course, once the diagram and threat model is created in your IriusRisk instance, all of the threat model data is stored in your instance. Does AI learn from my threat model? keyboard_arrow_down No, neither IriusRisk nor OpenAI learns from the use of AI, especially between customers. We may in future enable our AI functionality to learn, but it will purely remain in the context of the customer, and never shared between customers. Our use of OpenAI Enterprise means that your data is not used to train and enhance their LLMs. Is there a SLA for Jeff keyboard_arrow_down As of June 12th, OpenAI does not offer a Service Level Agreement (SLA) for latency (or any other) guarantees on their various engines, as indicated in their Help Center: Is there an SLA for latency guarantees on the various engines? | OpenAI Help Center. Consequently, we currently do not have an alternative service to ensure continuous availability if OpenAI services experience downtime. How do you ensure alignment in Jeff's responses? keyboard_arrow_down We have integrated technology within Jeff that rechecks the responses and their format before presenting them to the user. This includes: Focused Content: Jeff will only discuss topics related to Threat Modeling. Component Verification: Jeff will verify that the components used are actual IriusRisk components. Response Format: Jeff will ensure the format of the response is correct. Quality Control: Jeff does not assess the quality of the response; this is the responsibility of the human in the loop. These measures aim to enhance Jeff's reliability, though they are not 100% foolproof. What's the process if a response is not adequate? keyboard_arrow_down If you encounter an inadequate response from Jeff, please follow these steps: Note the Date and Time: Record the date and time of the response. Contact Customer Support: Reach out to our customer support team with the details. Conversation Review: With your permission, we will retrieve the conversation ID from OpenAI to study the interaction. Continuous Improvement: We will use the example to enhance Jeff's future performance and behavior. Your feedback helps us improve Jeff to better meet your needs. Are there any limitations on Jeff usage? keyboard_arrow_down We want software to be built securely from the design phase, this is why we have blazed a trail with our AI capabilities. More meaningful threat models equals more security by design. We are, therefore, not currently restricting the use of Jeff. There is however a fair use policy to ensure that nobody spoils it for the rest of us. But if you want to create meaningful threat models you will be all good with this fair use policy. Once again please contact your customer success manager who can allay any fears you may have. Is there a legal document for Jeff? keyboard_arrow_down Yes, there is a specific legal document called the AI Feature Addendum to the IriusRisk Customer Subscription Terms [Template] - Google Docs. This addendum outlines the terms and conditions related specifically to the use of Jeff. You can access and review this document through this link: AI Feature Addendum to the IriusRisk Customer Subscription Terms [Template] - Google Docs. Please refer to this document to understand the legal aspects and any obligations or rights concerning the use of Jeff. Where is the OpenAI instance located, and can we have any control over its location? Can we use our own Azure OpenAI instance? keyboard_arrow_down The OpenAI instance that powers Jeff is located in Ireland. Due to the use of exclusive OpenAI features specific to our implementation, it is not compatible with Azure GPT-4. As such, we cannot switch to a customer's Azure OpenAI instance. What is Jeff doing during the moments in the conversation when it appears inactive? keyboard_arrow_down During the demo, you might notice moments when Jeff seems inactive, but it's actively processing several critical tasks. Here's what's happening behind the scenes: Part I: Input Scenario – After you input a scenario, Jeff sends this data to the LLM for initial processing. Part II: Analysis and Diagram Generation – Jeff takes the scenario and: Analyzes key elements, Generates a preliminary diagram based on the scenario, Creates generic components. During this time, Jeff might seem inactive, but it is performing complex analysis and generation tasks. The user is then prompted to review and can make any necessary changes, which triggers these steps anew. Part III: Component Matching – Once the diagram is approved: Jeff requests information about components from the IriusRisk database, It then matches these to the generic components created in the previous step, A refined diagram is generated. This part of the process involves significant data processing, which might not be immediately apparent. Part IV: Finalization – After the user approves the final diagram, Jeff sends it to IR for completion, including threat modeling. Jeff then waits for the entire process to finalize, which can also appear as an inactive period. How much does it cost to add Jeff to my IriusRisk instance? keyboard_arrow_down We will absorb the cost of the OpenAI usage for the first year. Do you check or change the text sent to OpenAI (for example, PII filtering or any other kind of change)? keyboard_arrow_down No, we do not modify or filter the text sent to OpenAI. We only verify the format of the text sent from the user to the LLM to ensure it meets the required structure. Jeff's Origin. Why Jeff? Marketing would like to state that the christening of Jeff (IriusRisk's AI) came from a concern that the anthropomorphization of AI bots was a risk and constant worry. That somehow this would render the bot to be synonymous with the Terminator movies and fictitious creations of that ilk - not a tool but a competitor. To overcome this we subverted the form by choosing such a normal name so as to render the human-like nature null and void. Imbued with the Ancient Greek principle of bathos of turning the sublime into the trivial and ridiculous. To take the current height of human achievement (AI) and subvert it with the name of a middle-aged man anyone of us could know. We would like to state the above, but we cannot. Someone suggested the name Jeff, we all liked it and went to the pub. We then had to justify the decision to the wider team (see paragraph one). You've reached the end of the page... You must be interested Jeff is interesting, we get it. Explore how Jeff can help supercharge your threat modeling as well as the other capabilities IriusRisk can provide.
